## costs

### opportunity

When doing a certain thing, that thing's ⟮c:1;opportunity cost⟯ is ⟮c:2;the loss of value/benefit⟯ from ⟮c:3;anything one could do instead (and has to forgo⟯)

### sunk

A ⟮c:1;sunk cost⟯ is a cost that ⟮c:2;has already been incurred and cannot be recovered⟯, in contrast, ⟮s:1-2;a ((c:3;::prospective cost⟯ is a cost that ⟮c:4;can be avoided if action is taken⟯))

### other types

<img src="240px-CVP-TC-FC-VC.svg.png">⟮h:all;<img src="Marginal_cost1a.png">⟯
<br>⟮c:1;Fixed costs⟯ ⟮c:2;are the costs that are not dependent on the quantity of goods or services provided⟯.
In contrast, ⟮c:3;variable costs⟯ ⟮c:4;increase as the quantity of goods or services produced/provided increase⟯.
⟮c:5;Together, fixed costs and variable costs make up⟯ ⟮c:6;total cost⟯, which is ⟮c:7;the cost of producing some quantity of output⟯.
The ⟮c:8;marginal cost⟯ of something is ⟮c:9;the cost of producing additional quantity⟯
⟮c:10;Marginal cost⟯ is either measured in ⟮c:11;one extra unit⟯, or in ⟮c:12;a graph for infinitesimal amounts⟯
The ⟮c:13;marginal cost⟯ is calculated by ⟮c:14;dividing⟯ ⟮c:15;the change in total cost⟯ ⟮c:14;by⟯ ⟮c:15;the change in total quantity⟯
The ⟮c:16;average cost⟯ of something is ⟮c:17;the total cost⟯ ⟮c:19;divided by⟯ ⟮c:18;the number of goods produced.⟯

### externalities

An ⟮c:1;externality⟯ is a ⟮c:2;s:3;cost⟯ ( = ⟮c:3;s:2;negative⟯ ⟮c:6;s:1;externality⟯) or ⟮c:2;s:3;benefit⟯ ( = ⟮c:3;s:2;positive⟯ ⟮c:6;s:1;externality⟯) caused by ⟮c:5;a producer⟯ that is ⟮c:4;not incurred or received by that producer.⟯

## goods and services

### price

#### asset price bubble

⟮c:1;Economic bubbles⟯ are about ⟮c:2;asset prices⟯.
In ⟮c:3;an economic bubble⟯, ⟮c:4;asset price⟯ ⟮c:5;far exeeds⟯ ⟮c:4;it's 'real' value⟯.
An economic bubble ⟮c:6;bursts⟯ when ⟮c:7;the asset price crashes.⟯
Both ⟮c:8;the growth⟯ and ⟮c:8;subsequent decline⟯ in asset prices in an economic bubble is usually ⟮c:9;quite fast⟯.

### goods

#### commodities

Commodity is a term with three competing definitions:
  <thead>name|field|definition|examples
  <tbody class="cloze-group-children hide-if-inactive-children">
⟮c:1;commodity⟯|⟮c:2;economics⟯|⟮c:3;good with full/high degree of fungibility⟯|⟮c:4;s:all;wheat, petroleum, copper, rice, but not handmade bracelets⟯
⟮c:5;commodity⟯|⟮c:6;marxism⟯|⟮c:7;something with both use and exchange value⟯
⟮c:8;commodity⟯|⟮c:9;casual usage⟯|⟮c:10;any kind of market good⟯

From these definitions, ⟮c:11;commodification/commoditization⟯ may refer to ⟮c:12;any of the three definitions of commodity (economics, marxism, casual usage⟯)

#### fungibility

If a good is ⟮c:1;fungible⟯, it is ⟮c:2;readily interchanged with others of its kind⟯ without ⟮c:3;that making much of a difference⟯.
⟮c:4;Money, natural resources of the same grade, stocks of the same company⟯ all are examples for things with a high degree of ⟮c:5;fungibility⟯.

#### distribution

##### positional goods

a ⟮c:1;positional good⟯ is a good that is ⟮c:2;only valued⟯ by ⟮c:3;how it is distributed in a population⟯.
⟮c:4;Positional good⟯ is a term similar to ⟮c:5;status symbol.⟯
  {{c6::Conspicuous comsumption}} is consumption to {{c7::enhance social status/prestige}}, that is, consumption {{c8::of goods}} as {{c8::positional goods}}

#### durability

A ⟮c:1;durable⟯ or ⟮c:2;hard⟯ good is a good that ⟮c:3;does not quickly wear out or, more specifically, one that yields utility over time rather than being completely consumed in one use⟯ (Examples: ⟮c:4;s:3;a bicycle, washing machine, or ring⟯ ).
A ⟮c:5;non-durable⟯ or ⟮c:6;soft⟯ good is either defined as ⟮c:7;a good that is either consumed in one use⟯ (Examples: ⟮c:8;s:7;food, drinks, tissues⟯ ) or ⟮c:9;a good that has a lifespan of less than three years⟯ (Examples: ⟮c:10;s:9;cosmetics, medication, clothing⟯).

#### excludability and rivalrousness

##### excludability

A good that is ⟮c:1;excludable⟯ ⟮c:2;can have its use limited to certain people⟯. Examples: ⟮c:3;s:2;cinemas, food⟯
examples for ⟮c:4;non-excludable⟯ goods: ⟮c:5;air, public roads⟯.
As argued by ⟮c:6;Ostrom⟯, in fact excludability is a ⟮c:7;scale⟯ ⟮c:8;from being able to fully limit people from its use to not at all.⟯

##### rivalrousness

<br>---<br>.
A good is ⟮c:1;rival(rous⟯) if ⟮c:2;consumption by one party prevents consumption by another⟯
Examples for ⟮c:13;rivalrous goods⟯ include ⟮c:3;an item of food, a hammer, a domain name⟯.
A good is ⟮c:4;non-rival(rous⟯) if ⟮c:5;consumption by another induces no additional cost⟯
Examples for ⟮c:14;non-rivalrous goods⟯ ⟮c:6;a television broadcast, a scenic view, clean air⟯.
A good is ⟮c:7;anti-rival(rous⟯) if ⟮c:8;consumption by another provides benefits to other consumers⟯, generally via ⟮c:9;network effects⟯.
Examples of ⟮c:15;anti-rivalrous good⟯s include ⟮c:10;FOSS software, social media, a specific language⟯
We may want to define ⟮c:11;a scale⟯ ⟮c:12;from rival(rous) through non-rival(rous) to anti-rival(rous⟯).

A ⟮c:1;network effect⟯ is when ⟮c:2;the value of a good/service increases with the amount of users⟯. Examples: ⟮c:3;Social networks, the phone or the internet⟯.
The precise value of a ⟮c:8;network effect⟯ is often stated in terms of ⟮c:4;Metcalfe's law⟯: ⟮c:5;value of network⟯ = ⟮c:6;(amount of users)<sup>2</sup>⟯. This law has since recieved some ⟮c:7;empirical validation⟯ for e.g. ⟮c:7;facebook, bitcoin⟯

###### common pool resources

A ⟮c:1;common-pool resource⟯ is a good that is ⟮c:2;non-excludable⟯ and ⟮c:2;rivalrous⟯, e.g. ⟮c:3;pasture⟯.
A ⟮c:5;common-pool resource⟯ may be subject to ⟮c:4;the tragedy of the commons⟯ (however, not necessarily)
In ⟮c:6;the tragedy of the commons⟯, ⟮c:7;any individual⟯ has ⟮c:7;an incentive to⟯ {{c8::(over)exploit the resource}.
In ⟮c:9;the tragedy of the commons⟯, ⟮c:10;the resource is depleted⟯ due the overexploitation.
As proven by ⟮c:11;Elinor Ostrom⟯ in the work that won her ⟮c:12;the 2009 economics nobel prize⟯, ⟮c:13;commons/common-pool resources⟯ can ⟮c:14;effectively be managed by their communities⟯ if ⟮c:15;certain rules/principles are followed⟯, preventing ⟮c:16;the tragedy of the commons⟯

###### public goods

A good that is ⟮c:1;non-excludable⟯ (= ⟮c:1;use cannot be prevented⟯) and ⟮c:2;non-rival(rous⟯) (= ⟮c:2;use does not reduce availability⟯) is a ⟮c:3;public good⟯.
Examples of ⟮c:9;public goods⟯ are ⟮c:4;National security, lighthouses, knowledge⟯
⟮c:10;public goods⟯ are subject to the ⟮c:5;free rider⟯ problems, where ⟮c:6;free riders⟯ ⟮(c:6;people who use the good but don't contribute to it⟯) result in ⟮c:7;the good being under-produced or overused and degraded⟯.
Examples of public goods ⟮c:11;subject to free rider problems⟯: ⟮c:8;Herd immunity, grades of group-graded projects⟯
A ⟮c:1;free rider⟯ is someone who ⟮c:2;recieves a benefit⟯ ⟮c:3;without contributing adequately to its cost of production⟯.

###### excludable goods

A ⟮c:1;club good⟯ is a good that is ⟮c:2;excludable⟯ and ⟮c:2;non-rivalrous⟯, e.g. ⟮c:3;toll roads⟯
A ⟮c:4;private good⟯ is a good that is ⟮c:5;excludable⟯ and ⟮c:5;rivalrous⟯, e.g. ⟮c:6;cheese⟯

### bias

⟮c:1;Bias⟯ is the ⟮c:2;inclination toward⟯ or ⟮c:2;weighting of⟯ something, most often in a way that is ⟮c:3;unfair⟯ and/or ⟮c:3;disproportionate/wrong⟯. Thus, ⟮s:1-3;((s:7-12;::((c:4;::cognitive bias⟯ is ⟮c:5;the disproportionate weighting⟯ / ⟮c:5;irrelevant inclusion⟯ of factors in a way that ⟮c:6;the resulting judgement departs from rationality⟯)); ⟮s:4-6,10-12;bias in ((c:7;::statistics⟯ is the tendency of ⟮c:8;a statistic, more specifically an estimator (a truth about a sample⟯) to ⟮c:9;misestimate⟯ a ⟮c:8;parameter (a truth about the population) ⟯));⟮s:4-9; ((c:10;::implicit bias⟯ are ⟮c:11;pre-reflective (or impossible-to-reflect-away) attitudes⟯ about ⟮c:12;a social group⟯)))) 
⟮c:14;Status quo bias⟯ is a form of ⟮c:15;cognitive bias⟯, the bias towards ⟮c:13;The current state of affairs⟯ 

#### bothsidesism

⟮c:1;Bothsidesing⟯ is a term where you ⟮c:4;present both sides of an argument with equal weight⟯ (or ⟮c:4;assign both parties equal fault⟯), even if ⟮c:5;one does actually merits more weight⟯ (e.g. ⟮c:5;is true, is more representative⟯) or ⟮c:5;have more fault⟯ 
⟮c:3;Bothesidesing⟯ as a term is generally used as ⟮c:6;a criticsim⟯, especially ⟮c:2;of the media.⟯ 

#### magical thinking

⟮c:6;magical thinking⟯ is the way of thinking that posits ⟮c:7;that two events are causally connected⟯, ⟮c:8;even though there is no plausible causal link⟯.
An example of ⟮c:2;magical thinking⟯ would be ⟮c:1;Knocking on wood will prevent misfortune.⟯ 
⟮c:3;Magical thinking⟯ is often ⟮c:4;a symptom of OCD⟯ (however, ⟮c:5;the cause of most⟯ ⟮c:3;magical thinking⟯ in the world is ⟮c:5;not⟯ ⟮c:4;OCD⟯)

### birth rate/fertility

The ⟮c:5;different fertility rates are⟯ sometimes presented ⟮c:6;as-is⟯ (as ⟮c:6;a percentage⟯), or ⟮c:7;multiplied by 1000⟯, to ⟮c:7;present a number/index⟯ 

What we often call ⟮c:8;birth rate⟯ is more properly called the ⟮c:9;crude birth rate⟯, because ⟮c:10;it isn't directly based on anything related to childbirth⟯ 
The ⟮c:11;crude birth rate⟯ is ⟮c:12;the number of live births⟯ ⟮c:13;per person⟯ 
To get ⟮c:14;the crude birth rate⟯ in practice, we ⟮c:15;divide the number of live births through the population size⟯ 

The ⟮c:16;general fertility rate⟯ is ⟮c:17;the amount of live births⟯ ⟮c:18;per women aged 15 to 44 (women of childbearing age⟯) 

The ⟮c:19;age-specific fertility rate⟯ is ⟮c:20;the number of births⟯ ⟮c:21;to women in a given age group⟯ ⟮c:22;divided by the number of women in a given age group⟯ 
The ⟮c:23;ASFR⟯ is often ⟮c:24;grouped in groups of⟯ ⟮c:25;1⟯ or ⟮c:25;5⟯ ⟮c:25;years⟯. 
In plain language, the ⟮c:26;ASFR⟯ e.g. for age 21 is ⟮c:27;the amount of children an average 21 y/o has in a year⟯ 

The ⟮c:28;TFR⟯ measures ⟮c:29;babies per woman (in her lifetime⟯) (*) 
Mathematically, the ⟮c:30;total fertility rate⟯ is ⟮c:31;the sum of all⟯ ⟮c:32;current age-specific fertility rates⟯ 
In plain language, the ⟮c:33;TFR⟯ is ⟮c:34;the sum of⟯ ⟮c:35;the amount of chilren⟯ ⟮c:36;a women will have⟯ ⟮c:37;in any given year of her life⟯ 
The thing the ⟮c:38;total fertility rate⟯ is ⟮c:39;trying to estimate⟯ is ⟮c:40;the amount of children a woman is expected to have in her lifetime (subject to some theoretical assumptions⟯) 
The ⟮c:41;total fertility rate⟯ assumes that ⟮c:42;the woman will survive until the end of her reproductive life⟯ and that ⟮c:43;she will to experience the exact current age-specific fertility rates (ASFRs) through her lifetime⟯ 
If a ⟮c:44;woman dies before her reproductive period ends (lets say at 30),⟯ she ⟮c:45;will not impact ASFRs beyond 30⟯. This is not a problem until ⟮c:46;we add them together to the TFR⟯ - since we are using the ASFRs to ⟮c:47;estimate how many children a woman will have in her life⟯, ⟮c:48;the ASFRs⟯ ⟮c:49;should be impacted b⟯⟮c:50;y the woman who died before the end of the reproductive period (the dead women should count as 0⟯). Therefore, ⟮c:51;maternal mortality distorts the TFR⟯. 


The ⟮c:52;replacement rate / replacement-level fertility⟯ is ⟮c:53;the TFR⟯ ⟮c:54;needed to sustain population levels⟯ 
A ⟮c:56;TFR⟯ ⟮c:55;below replacement rate⟯ is known as ⟮c:57;Sub-replacement fertility⟯ 
Beyond ⟮c:58;the distorting factors for the TFR⟯, ⟮c:59;the replacement rate⟯ is additionally distorted by ⟮c:60;child mortality⟯. 
⟮c:61;The replacement rate⟯ for countries ⟮c:62;with low child and maternal mortality⟯ is ⟮c:63;about 2.1⟯.

### dependency

#### definition of dependent

In demographics/economics, a ⟮c:1;dependent⟯ is someone ⟮c:2;who relies on someone else for financial support.⟯
For statistical purposes, it is generally assumed that ⟮c:3;those aged 15-64⟯ are ⟮c:4;non-dependents⟯, and ⟮c:3;those aged 0-14 and 65-⟯ are ⟮c:4;dependents⟯.
The relationship of ⟮c:6;dependents per non-dependent⟯ is expressed ⟮c:5;in the dependency ratio⟯.

#### dependency ratio

The ⟮c:1;dependency ratio (amount of people who work divided by people who can't⟯) is used as a proxy for ⟮c:2;pressure on the productive population⟯,
A ⟮c:3;lower⟯ ⟮c:20;dependency ratio⟯ is generally better because it often results in ⟮c:4;economic growth⟯.
When ⟮c:5;a change to a lower dependency ratio leads to economic growth⟯, which is most often caused when there is a ⟮c:6;high fertility rate⟯, resulting in ⟮c:6;a bunch of young people⟯, followed by ⟮c:7;a decrease in fertility⟯, and often ⟮c:8;augmented by an increase in life expectancy⟯, this is termed a ⟮c:9;demographic dividend⟯.
the idea of a ⟮c:21;demographic dividend⟯ is used to explain e.g. ⟮c:10;China's rapid economic growth⟯ - in ⟮c:11;2010⟯ they had an unprecedented ⟮c:12;dependency ratio⟯ of ⟮c:13;35%⟯, at the a time where the world average was ⟮c:13;53%⟯.
Of course, ⟮c:14;this large group of young people⟯ responsible for a ⟮c:23;demographic dividend⟯ will ⟮c:22;eventually grow old⟯, with ⟮c:14;less young people to support them⟯, leading to ⟮c:15;an increased dependency ratio⟯. this has been termed the ⟮c:16;demographic tax⟯ or ⟮c:16;demographic burden⟯.
A ⟮c:24;demographic burden/tax results⟯ in ⟮c:17;lower economic growth⟯ - a fact ⟮c:18;Japan⟯ ⟮c:19;is currently experiencing⟯ and ⟮c:18;China⟯ ⟮c:19;probably will fall victim to as well⟯.

### sex/gender

#### missing women

The term "⟮c:1;missing women⟯" (coined by ⟮c:8;Amartya Sen⟯) indicates a shortfall in ⟮c:2;the number of women⟯ relative to ⟮c:2;the expected number of women⟯ in ⟮c:7;a region or country⟯. 
⟮c:10;missing women⟯ are often measured/discovered through ⟮c:3;male-to-female sex ratios⟯, specifically via ⟮c:11;the deviance from⟯ the ⟮c:12;natural roughly 1:1 m:f ratio (in fact, in the west it is generally 0.98:1⟯) 
⟮c:13;Sen⟯ argued that there were about ⟮c:14;100 million⟯ ⟮c:15;"missing women"⟯ in ⟮c:16;Asia⟯. 
The phenomenon of ⟮c:9;missing women⟯ and is theorized to be caused by ⟮c:4;sex-selective abortions⟯, ⟮c:5;female infanticide⟯, and ⟮c:6;inadequate healthcare and nutrition for female children⟯. 
The fact of the existence of missing women is caused by ⟮c:17;sexism⟯, and may be seen as a pretty direct expression of ⟮c:18;their -value status.⟯

## political spectrum

### overton window

⟮h:all;<img src="sm_paste-d320cd7f7b28b6755889aacc46fa08635d6a2262.jpg">⟯⟮c:1;The overton window⟯ is the concept that there is ⟮c:4;a range of ideas/policies⟯ that are ⟮c:5;acceptable⟯ at ⟮c:6;any given time⟯, which then ⟮c:7;determines the acceptability/radicalness of other ideas⟯ ⟮c:8;in relation to it⟯. 
In the overton window concept, everything ⟮c:9;inside the window⟯ is considered to be ⟮c:2;acceptable/reasonable/sensible⟯ 
In the overton window concept, everything ⟮c:10;outside the window⟯ is considered to be ⟮c:3;radical/unthinkable⟯ 
⟮c:11;Introducing radical ideas⟯ ⟮c:12;shifts the overton windon into that direction⟯. 
To make something ⟮c:13;viable/actual policy⟯, first ⟮c:14;the overton window⟯ needs to be ⟮c:15;shifted in that direction.⟯

## international relations

### irredentism & revanchism

{{c2::Irredentism}} is the movement to {{c3::'reclaim' territory}} that a group thinks {{c4::they have a claim to}} on a {{c5::ethno-historico-nationalist}} basis. 
{{c6::Revanchism}} is the movement to {{c7::reclaim territory}} {{c8::lost to another}} due to {{c9::past events}}. 
Both {{c10::irredentism}} and {{c10::revanchism}} see their {{c11::territorial claims}} as a {{c12::reclaiming}}, though the {{c13::motivation/ideology behind it}} differs. 
{{c14::revanchism}} is like {{c14::irredentism}}, but generally the territory claimed {{c1::was once actually held}}, with less emphasis on {{c1::whether that territory is ethnically/'properly' part of the nation}} 


France ⟮c:17;claiming alsace-lorraine/wanting it back⟯ was both a ⟮c:18;irredentist⟯ and a ⟮c:19;revanchist⟯ claim. 
An ⟮c:20;irredentist⟯ claim is often expressed as a claim for ⟮c:15;Greater X (Greater Serbia etc.⟯) 
the ⟮c:21;Megali Idea (Great Idea⟯) was an ⟮c:22;irredentist (though probably not revanchist⟯) claim by ⟮c:23;greece⟯ 
The term ⟮c:24;irredentism⟯ comes from ⟮c:25;italia irredenta⟯ (= ⟮c:24;irredentism⟯ related ⟮c:26;to italians in dalmatia, corsica, etc.⟯) 
The etymological roots of irredentism is that the territory is ⟮c:16;not redeemed (from italian Italia irredenta⟯)

### place / time

#### US

In American politics, the ⟮c:1;Southern strategy⟯ was a ⟮c:2;Republican Party⟯ electoral strategy to increase political support ⟮c:3;among white voters in the South⟯ by ⟮c:4;appealing to racism against African Americans⟯ starting during (and exploiting the fears created by⟮c:5; the civil rights era of the 1950s and 1960s⟯), causing the ⟮s:2;Republican party⟯ to end up ⟮c:6;far further to the right and in roughly the same position as it has today⟯. Politicians most associated with this are ⟮c:7;Richard Nixon⟯ and ⟮c:8;Barry Goldwater.⟯ 

⟮c:1;Lee Atwater⟯ has a famous ⟮c:2;quote⟯ in which he describes the evolution of ⟮c:4;republican::whose?⟯ messaging in the context of the ⟮c:3;southern strategy⟯ from ⟮c:5;"Nigger"⟯ to ⟮c:6;dog-whistles⟯ such as ⟮c:6;forced busing⟯ and ⟮c:7;state's rights⟯ to ⟮c:8;supposedly economic issues⟯ such as ⟮c:8;cutting taxes⟯ that ⟮c:9;"just happen" to hurt black people more⟯. 
⟮c:11;Lee Atwater'⟯s famous quote is often understood as ⟮c:10;the intentional perfidiousness of hiding racism in structural violence not mentioning race⟯, but he actually believed that ⟮c:10;this would end up making race less important⟯. 

> You start out in 1954 by saying, "Nigger, nigger, nigger." By 1968, you can't say "nigger" – that hurts you. Backfires. So you say stuff like forced busing, states' rights and all that stuff. You're getting so abstract now, you're talking about cutting taxes. And all these things you're talking about are totally economic things and a byproduct of them is [that] blacks get hurt worse than whites. And subconsciously maybe that is part of it. I'm not saying that. But I'm saying that if it is getting that abstract, and that coded, that we are doing away with the racial problem one way or the other. You follow me – because obviously sitting around saying, "We want to cut this," is much more abstract than even the busing thing, and a hell of a lot more abstract than "Nigger, nigger."

ARPA is short for ⟮c:1;Advanced Research Projects Agency⟯ (today called ⟮c:2;DARPA⟯), and is an agency that carries out ⟮c:3;R&amp;D⟯, directly subsidiary to the ⟮c:4;US Department of Defense⟯

The novel ⟮c:1;Molloy⟯ is by ⟮c:2;Samuel Beckett⟯, it features the (moderately) well-known '⟮c:3;pebble scene⟯'. 
In the pebble scene, the protagonist has ⟮c:5;a jacket⟯ and ⟮c:5;a pair of pants⟯ with ⟮c:5;four pockets total⟯ and ⟮c:6;16⟯ ⟮c:6;pebbles⟯. 
In the pebble scene, the protagonist wants to ⟮c:4;suck all 16 pebbles⟯ without ⟮c:7;repeating one⟯ 
In the pebble scene, neither does the protagonist think it is possible ⟮c:8;to have 16 pockets⟯, nor does he want to ⟮c:9;have all 16 pebbles in one pocket, due to the imbalance⟯.

###### harlequin

⟮h:all;<img src="sm_51VaV4puTsL._SX297_BO1,204,203,200_.jpg">⟯§§ ⟮c:1;Harlequin Enterprises⟯ is a company that ⟮c:2;publishes romance novels⟯, and reached a peak market domination (in NA) of ⟮c:3;80-90%⟯ of sales in ⟮c:4;the 1990s⟯. As of 2002, they publish about a ⟮c:5;1k books⟯ a year. §<br>
⟮c:6;Harlequin⟯ sets ⟮c:7;precise standards⟯ for their romance novels, with ⟮c:8;restrictive contracts⟯ with ⟮c:9;bad pay⟯ and ⟮c:10;precise page counts⟯. 
⟮c:11;Harlequin⟯ features different ⟮c:12;series⟯ with different ⟮c:12;themes⟯, which also vary things such as ⟮c:13;sexual content⟯. 
Harlequin series include <cite>⟮c:14;Love Inspired⟯</cite> ⟮(c:15;Christian romances where faith is featured as prominently as passion⟯) and <cite>⟮c:16;Harlequin Presents⟯</cite> ("a⟮c:17;lpha males, decadent glamour and jet-set lifestyles⟯") 
According to Radway (1984), ⟮c:18;the harlequin hero⟯ is s⟮c:19;pectacularly masculine, well-built, etc.⟯ in appearance, ⟮c:20;indifferent/bad-tempered⟯ in personality, but ⟮c:21;with a token indication of underlying softness (e.g. love of animals or children⟯)

###### japanese

According to Shibamoto Smith 2004, in contrast to ⟮c:1;Harlequin translations⟯, the ⟮c:2;heroine⟯ of ⟮c:1;japanese romance novels⟯ looks ⟮c:3;reasonably pretty, but not a bombshell⟯, acts ⟮c:4;independently/competently⟯, is quite ⟮c:5;self-aware⟯, while the ⟮c:6;hero⟯ is ⟮c:7;ordinary, nice, somewhat awkward⟯. 
In general, according to Shibamoto Smith 2004, ⟮c:8;japanese romance novels⟯ pay less heed to ⟮c:9;looks as a sign for attractiveness/love⟯

Japanese romances rarely have covers that depict the principal characters, and the text descriptions are by no means as detailed as in the Harlequin-style texts. Clothing is not, in my corpus, ever used as a tool of seduction, and no lovers’ furtive glances at escaping chest hair or heaving breasts or quick but intoxicating looks at glimpses of beautiful long legs through the slits of skirts help our Japanese lovers know that they have found Ms. or Mr. Right

### journalism

#### newswriting

##### structure

The ⟮c:1;introductory section/opening paragraph⟯ is known as ⟮c:2;the lede⟯, especially in ⟮c:3;news writing⟯.  
In general, it is said the lede ⟮c:4;should contain⟯ ⟮c:5;the most newsworthy/interesting part⟯, and ⟮s:gb;((c:6;::if an article does not have the most newsworthy part in the lede⟯, it is said to be ⟮c:7;burying the lede⟯)). 
If we're reporting a house fire, and we mention the location, time and cause of the fire before mentioning the two deaths, what are we probably doing?  ⟮c:8;Burying the lede⟯ 
Nora Ephron had an assignment to write a <b>opening</b> for a news story about t<b>eachers going to speak</b> in a neighboring city on <b>thursday</b> for the <b>school paper</b>. What (according to her teacher) is the <b>lede</b> for this story?  ⟮c:8;There will be no school next thursday.⟯ 
A doctor is giving a speech about fad diets and physical fitness to a group of local businesspeople. Midway through his speech, the good doctor collapses of a heart attack. He dies en route to the hospital. What should definitely be  in the lede? ⟮c:9;the death (and prob the heartattack⟯)

##### screenplay

⟮c:1;Fountain⟯ is  a markup language for ⟮c:3;screenplays⟯ inspired by ⟮c:2;markdown⟯ 

###### title page

In fountain, a ⟮c:1;title page⟯ consists of ⟮c:2;key-values pairs⟯, ⟮h:gb;separated internally by ((c:3;:::⟯.)) 
In fountain's title page you can have ⟮c:4;multiple of the same key⟯. the value can ⟮c:5;stretch over multiple lines⟯, ⟮h:gb;if it does, ((c:6;::lines beyond the first⟯ should be ⟮c:7;preceded by 3 or more spaces, or a tab⟯. )) 
In fountain, the ⟮c:8;title page⟯ is optional, but must be ⟮c:9;the first thing in the document if it appears⟯. 

###### parantheticals

In screenplays, ⟮c:1;parantheticals⟯ are ⟮c:2;the annotations in parentheses⟯, generally ⟮c:3;between⟯ ⟮c:4;a characters name⟯ and ⟮c:4;their dialogue⟯. 
⟮c:5;parathenticals⟯ are ⟮c:6;centered⟯, as are ⟮c:7;the character name⟯, and ⟮c:8;the dialogue after.⟯ 
In screenplays, ⟮c:9;characters names⟯ are written in ⟮c:10;all caps⟯ when ⟮c:11;above dialogue elements⟯ and when ⟮c:12;they first appear⟯, and ⟮c:13;in normal case afterwards⟯. 
In fountain, an ⟮c:14;all-caps line⟯ automatically becomes ⟮c:15;a charater element⟯ ⟮(c:16;a character name before dialogue⟯) unless ⟮c:17;it has a good reason not to⟯. 
In fountain, you indicate ⟮c:18;a paranthetical⟯ by ⟮c:19;surrounding it in (⟯). 
In fountain, anything ⟮c:20;after a paranthetical element⟯ or ⟮c:21;after a character element⟯ ⟮c:22;automatically becomes dialogue⟯. 
In fountain. ⟮c:23;a caret⟯ ⟮c:24;after a character name⟯ indicates that ⟮c:25;this is dialogue simultaneous/dual/parallel⟯. 

###### action text

⟮h:all;<img src="sm_paste-93acbcc29329144602ed62e027beb9100c001b91.jpg">⟯§§ In screenplays,  ⟮c:6;text describing what is happening in a scene⟯ is called ⟮c:1;action (text⟯). it is normally in ⟮c:7;normal case.⟯ §<br>
⟮c:2;Any text that fountain doesn't think is something else⟯ is ⟮c:3;action text⟯.  
Within ⟮c:4;action text⟯, fountain ⟮c:5;preserves your whitespace choices⟯ 

###### scene headings

The ⟮c:7;line⟯ ⟮c:8;starting a scene⟯ is called ⟮c:9;a scene heading⟯. 
A ⟮c:10;scene heading⟯ is usually in ⟮c:11;all caps⟯. 
§§ ⟮c:12;Scene headings⟯ tell you ⟮c:13;outsideness⟯, ⟮c:14;location⟯, and ⟮c:15;time⟯. §<br><div class="cloze-group hide-if-inactive">A ⟮c:6;scene heading⟯ usually looks sth like this:
  ```lang=text;

```</div>
In fountain, ⟮c:16;scene headings⟯ are ⟮c:17;separated from the rest of the text⟯ by ⟮c:18;blank lines⟯. 
In fountain scene headings ⟮c:19;must start with⟯ ⟮c:20;EXT/INT etc.⟯ unless forced 
§§ In fountain, you can indicate ⟮c:21;scene numbers⟯ by ⟮c:22;wrapping them in #foo#⟯, they are placed at ⟮c:23;the end of scene headings⟯ §<br>

## tools

### cardinal directions & compasses

<img src="sm_italy-map-with-compass-rose-vector-id165747631.jpg">
<img src="sm_1280px-Windrose.svg.jpg"><br>A ⟮c:1;compass rose⟯ can be found on ⟮c:2;pretty much any compass⟯ and on ⟮c:3;many maps⟯. 
The ⟮c:4;compas rose⟯ indicates ⟮c:5;where the cardinal directions are⟯, ⟮c:6;in relation to⟯ ⟮c:7;the thing on which it appears⟯. 
The ⟮c:8;four⟯ ⟮c:10;main directions⟯, ⟮c:9;90 deg⟯ apart, that a compass rose has are ⟮c:11;the cardinal directions⟯. 
The ⟮c:12;four⟯ directions e⟮c:13;xactly in the middle between the cardinal directions⟯ are the ⟮c:14;intercardinal directions⟯. 
The ⟮c:15;intercardinal directions⟯ have the syntax ⟮c:16;&lt;north/south&gt;&lt;east/west&gt;⟯ 
The ⟮c:17;eight directions between the intercardinal directions⟯ I will term ⟮c:18;interintercardinal directions⟯. 
The ⟮c:19;interintercardinal directions⟯ have the syntax ⟮c:20;&lt;cardinal-direction&gt;&lt;intercardinal-direction&gt;⟯&nbsp;, where the intercardinal direction and the cardinal are ⟮c:21;those they are closest to⟯. 
The sixteen directions ⟮c:22;between the interintercardinal directions⟯ I wil term ⟮c:23;interinterintercardinal directions⟯. 
Generating the ⟮c:24;interinterintercardinal directions⟯ follows the following algorithm:
```
let iiicString = ⟮c:25;iic.closest(cardinal or intercardinal direcction⟯)
⟮c:26;iicString += "by"⟯
if ⟮c:27;iic.isClockwiseOf(closest(cardinal or intercardinal direcction⟯))
  ⟮c:28;iicString += closestClockwise(cardinal direction⟯)
else 
  ⟮c:29;iicString += closesCounterclockwise(cardinal direction⟯)
```

## integrated geography

### disasters

Disasters<br/>While it might seem obvious to ⟮c:1;evacuate⟯ in the face of a storm, it may be ⟮c:2;difficult⟯ due to ⟮c:3;traffic blockages⟯ or ⟮c:4;economic considerations⟯ 
Since ⟮c:5;you may not be able to wash things for a while⟯ once a storm (e.g. hurricane, severe flooding, etc.) hits, ⟮c:6;wash/clean everything you can beforehand⟯ 
Since ⟮c:7;ATMs and electronic payment may not work⟯, having ⟮c:8;enough cash⟯ is important in an emergency situation 
Regardless of where you end up ⟮c:9;weathering a storm⟯, it's good to ⟮c:10;pack distractions⟯, especially ⟮c:11;non-electronic⟯ ones (or ⟮c:12;powerbanks⟯ etc.), to ⟮c:13;stave off boredom⟯ and ⟮c:14;keep stress-levels down⟯ 
Only use ⟮c:15;generators⟯ ⟮c:16;outside⟯ and ⟮c:17;away from⟯ your home/the space you are staying. 
One of the ⟮c:18;first things⟯ to do if ⟮c:19;you find out that a disaster will hit⟯ is ⟮c:20;find out if you should evacuate⟯. 
Many countries will have predefined ⟮c:21;evacuation routes⟯ for evacuation, which you ⟮c:22;should follow if possible⟯ 
In the US at least, for storms such as hurricanes there are ⟮c:23;voluntary⟯ and ⟮c:24;mandatory⟯ ⟮c:25;evacuation orders⟯. 
In response to an ⟮c:26;emergency⟯, ideally ⟮c:27;stay up to date⟯ on ⟮c:28;current goverment information⟯ 
If you ⟮c:29;evacuate⟯, make sure you take ⟮c:30;the necessary supplies&nbsp;⟯ 
If you decide to ⟮c:31;evacuate⟯ in response to an emergency, inform ⟮c:32;friends and family⟯ ⟮c:33;where you're going⟯ 
If you are subject to a severe storm/etc. and are staying home, ⟮c:34;fill up your bathtub with water⟯. It's not for ⟮c:35;drinking⟯, it's for ⟮c:36;flushing the toilet etc⟯. 
If you are subject to a severe storm/etc. and are staying home, perhaps the most important thing is to have ⟮c:37;water⟯ and ⟮c:38;non-perishable food⟯. 
Depending on the type of disaster and the government response, ⟮c:39;evacuation⟯ may be ⟮c:40;to predefined shelters⟯, but you also may be ⟮c:41;required or forced to find your own accomodation⟯ 
In general, in preparation for a storm, have at least ⟮c:42;3 l * 3 days⟯ of ⟮c:43;water⟯ per ⟮c:44;person⟯, ideally ⟮c:45;more⟯, plus ⟮c:46;some for washing hands etc.⟯ 
If you are staying home before a disaster hits, ⟮c:47;turn your fridge and freezer⟯ to ⟮c:48;the coldest setting⟯. Once ⟮c:49;the disaster hits⟯, ⟮c:50;keep them closed as much as possible⟯. This is to ⟮c:51;preserve the food as long as possible⟯. 
If you are in the path of a storm, ⟮c:52;fill up your gas tanks⟯, should you have a ⟮c:53;car⟯, so ⟮c:54;you can evacuate if necessary⟯. 
After&nbsp; a longer power outage, your ⟮c:55;food⟯ might be ⟮c:56;spoiled⟯. 
After a major catastrope you may ⟮c:57;be full of adrenaline⟯, so you might not ⟮c:58;notice huge injuries⟯ without ⟮c:59;checking yourself⟯.

## human geography

### heterotopia

⟮c:1;Heterotopia⟯ are ⟮c:4;similar to⟯ ⟮c:2;normal space⟯ in that they ⟮c:3;occupy space⟯, however they are ⟮c:4;different from⟯ ⟮c:2;normal space⟯ in a number of ways. 
In a very general sense, in ⟮c:5;heterotopia⟯ ⟮c:6;normal space⟯ is simultaneusly ⟮c:7;represented⟯, ⟮c:8;contested⟯ and ⟮c:9;inverted⟯ 
⟮c:10;Heterotopia⟯ often (related to their epistemic relation to real space) either ⟮c:11;create a space of illusory order⟯, or ⟮c:12;expose real space by contrast⟯ 
⟮c:13;Heterotopia⟯ are  often ⟮c:14;not accessible the way normal space is accessible⟯, they  either ⟮c:15;compell entrance (prisons etc.⟯) or ⟮c:16;require rituals/purification to enter⟯ ⟮c:17;Heterotopia⟯ have ⟮c:18;a function relative to society,⟯ amongst others ⟮c:19;heterotopia of crisis⟯ and  ⟮c:20;heterotopia of deviation⟯ 
Examples of ⟮c:21;heterotopia of crisis⟯ are ⟮c:22;boarding schools, honeymoon trips (for loss of virginity⟯) 
Examples of ⟮c:23;heterotopia of deviation⟯ are ⟮c:24;prisons, rest homes, psychiatric hospitals⟯ 
In modern society, ⟮c:25;heterotopia of crisis⟯ have the tendency to ⟮c:26;be replaced by heterotopia of deviation⟯ 
⟮c:27;Heterotopia⟯ have a close relationship with  the ⟮c:28;sacred (in a wide sense⟯) 
⟮c:29;Heterotopia⟯ often ⟮c:30;gather many different places in a single place⟯ (e.g.. gardens) ⟮c:31;Heterotopia⟯ often (related to time) correlate with ⟮c:32;heterochronia⟯, in that they either ⟮c:33;create a sort of achronia⟯ (e.g. ⟮c:33;museums⟯) or ⟮c:34;emphasize time in its most transitory⟯ (e.g. ⟮c:34;festivals⟯) 
All cultures have heterotopia, but ⟮c:35;the same heterotopia⟯ can have ⟮c:36;different functions⟯ ⟮c:37;in the same society at different times⟯, or ⟮c:38;in different societies⟯ 

### political geography

#### military geography

The ⟮c:2;chinese⟯ efforts to ⟮c:3;establish a series of friendly ports owned or beholden to them⟯ is called ⟮c:1;the String of Pearls initiative⟯ 
Some analysts have  alleged chinas true aims for ⟮c:5;the string of pearls initiative⟯ are ⟮c:4;Military bases⟯ 
China most often ⟮c:6;gains control of the ports⟯ for ⟮c:7;the string of pearls initiative⟯ ⟮c:8;via loans and the ports as collateral, which they collect when the goverments default⟯ 

#### nations & states

Currently there are ⟮c:5;193⟯ UN member states. 

##### capital cities

###### isolation and governance

According to <a href="https://www.nber.org/papers/w19028">Campante et al. 2013</a>, in ⟮c:1;authoritarian⟯ states ⟮c:2;capital cities⟯ being ⟮c:3;isolated⟯ correlates with ⟮c:4;worse quality of governance⟯ = ⟮c:4;more resource extraction (of the ruling elite from the country⟯). 
<a href="https://www.nber.org/papers/w19028">Campante et al. 2013</a> argue the relationship ⟮c:8;capital cities being more isolated -&gt; worse quality of government⟯ is ⟮c:5;causal⟯, based on a more ⟮c:6;isolated⟯ ⟮c:7;capital city⟯ being ⟮c:5;less succeptible to revolution⟯.

# history

## theories & approaches

### historical progress & value

#### relativisim & determinism

⟮c:1;Historical relativism⟯ is the idea that ⟮c:2;a period of / event in history⟯ can only be ⟮c:3;understood in its own terms⟯. 
⟮c:4;Historical determinism⟯ is the idea that ⟮c:5;history is determined in some way, e.g. by rules⟯. 
⟮c:6;Historism⟯ and ⟮c:6;historicism⟯ may refer to ⟮c:7;historical relativism⟯, ⟮c:7;historical determinism⟯, an idea where ⟮c:8;history should be priviledged as a cause⟯ for ⟮c:9;society⟯, ⟮c:9;culture⟯, or t⟮c:10;he explanation of phenomena⟯. Thus ⟮c:11;these terms are confused to the point of unusability.⟯ 

#### presentism

In history, ⟮c:1;presentism⟯ is the ⟮c:2;judgement/interpretation⟯ of the ⟮c:2;past⟯ in terms of ⟮c:3;current⟯ ⟮c:4;values⟯ and ⟮c:4;concepts⟯ 

#### combination

##### historical determinism

⟮c:1;Whig history⟯ at its most narrow sees ⟮c:2;british history⟯ as the ⟮c:3;inevitable⟯ ⟮c:3;progress⟯ from ⟮c:4;dark and despotic arbitrary tyranny⟯ to ⟮c:5;enlightened british liberal democracy⟯. 
⟮c:6;Whig history⟯ inbetween the most narrow and the most wide definitions sees ⟮c:7;history⟯ as the ⟮c:8;inevitable⟯ ⟮c:8;progress⟯ from ⟮c:9;a dark and terrible past of unfreedom⟯ to ⟮c:10;a glorious present of freedom and democracy⟯. 
⟮c:11;Whig history⟯ at its most broad sees ⟮c:12;history⟯ as ⟮c:13;the inevitable march of progress (where progress depends on the context it is used in, the author, etc.⟯). 



### historiography

⟮c:1;Historiography⟯ is the study of ⟮c:2;the way we write (and have written) history.⟯ 
The ⟮c:3;historiography⟯ of a specific thing is ⟮c:4;how history has studied/seen a specific thing⟯ 

#### black legend

a ⟮c:2;black legend⟯ is a ⟮c:3;historiographical⟯ phenomenon where ⟮c:4;an overly negative portrayal of something⟯ is created, often through ⟮c:1;biased writing⟯ 


### scales

#### microhistory

⟮c:1;Microhistory⟯ is a genre of history that ⟮c:2;focuses on small units of research⟯ 
Charles Joyner defines the questions ⟮c:3;microhistory⟯ asks as ⟮c:4;Ask large questions in small places⟯ 
The things ⟮c:5;microhistory⟯ might focus on are things such as ⟮c:6;an event, community, individual (esp. an unimportant one⟯) 
The ⟮c:7;most famous work of microhistory⟯ is  ⟮c:8;The cheese and the worms⟯ by ⟮c:9;Carlo Ginzburg⟯ 

#### macrohistory

⟮c:1;macrohistory⟯ is the approach to history ⟮c:2;looking for large, long-term trends⟯ in world history 

#### annales-school

The terms ⟮c:19;longue durée, moyenne durée and événement⟯ for scales of analysing history by ⟮c:20;the annales-school⟯ were formulated in their current form in ⟮c:21;the 1950s⟯ by  ⟮c:18;<span class="aside">Fernand⟯ Braudel</span> 
The three levels of history that ⟮c:22;the annales-school and fernand braudel⟯ distinguish are the ⟮c:23;longue durée⟯, ⟮c:24;moyenne durée⟯ and the ⟮c:25;événement⟯. 
The annales school favored ⟮c:26;the longue duree⟯ as a level of analysis

  <tbody class="cloze-group-children hide-if-inactive-children">⟮c:1,5-7;longue durée⟯|⟮c:2;s:3-7;hundreds or thousands of years⟯|⟮c:3;s:2-7;Changes in climatology or physical geography⟯||⟮c:4;s:2-7;Macrohistory⟯

⟮c:1;May You Live In Interesting Times⟯ (or variants) is a supposedly ⟮c:2;chinese⟯ quote, supposedly ⟮c:3;a curse⟯ due to the fact that ⟮c:4;the most interesting times are also the most dangerous⟯. however, ⟮c:5;the origin is unclear⟯. It could be ⟮c:6;a change from the Chinese quote "Truly, better be a dog in days of peace than a human in times of war"⟯, or ⟮c:7;an original creation of the british diplomatic service in china⟯.

⟮c:5;The deaths that John Snow mapped⟯ ⟮c:6;clustered around⟯ ⟮c:7;a certain pump.⟯ 
⟮c:8;The pump around which the deaths mapped by John Snow clustered's handle⟯ was then ⟮c:9;removed⟯. 
⟮c:10;John Snow's mapping effort⟯ is considered ⟮c:11;one of the founding methods⟯ of ⟮c:12;modern epidemioligy⟯. 

#### miasma and germ theory

The theory that ⟮c:2;bad air⟯ ⟮c:3;causes illness⟯ is called ⟮c:1;Miasma theory⟯ 
The ⟮c:4;miasma theory⟯ was popular until ⟮c:5;the 1880s⟯, when it was superceded by ⟮c:6;germ theory⟯. 
⟮c:7;The air thought to cause diseases under the miasma theory⟯ was called ⟮c:8;night air⟯ 
The ⟮c:9;miasma theory⟯ remains visible in the name of the illness ⟮c:10;malaria⟯

### slavery

#### soujorner truth

⟮c:31;Soujorner Truth⟯ was a ⟮c:32;former slave⟯ who became an ⟮c:33;abolitionist⟯ and ⟮c:33;women's right activist⟯ esp. ⟮c:34;from the 1840s onwards::time⟯ 
"⟮c:30;Ain't I a Woman⟯" mainly reflects on the theme of ⟮c:35;her being equal to men in any concievable way⟯, especially as regards ⟮c:36;physical endowments⟯ and ⟮c:37;the position in women in scripture⟯ (perhaps even ⟮c:38;more than equal⟯) 

The original version of "⟮c:1;Ain't I a Woman⟯" was published a ⟮c:2;few weeks⟯ after the speech and is probably ⟮c:3;relatively close to the original⟯, amongst other things because it doesn't suffer from ⟮c:4;imputing a southern accent to soujourner truth⟯, but was published in ⟮c:5;an anti-slavery magazine⟯ and thus ⟮c:6;had its own audience to consider⟯ 
The more commonly known version of Soujourner Truths speech "⟮c:7;Ain't I a woman⟯" was probably ⟮c:8;significantly changed⟯ by ⟮c:9;an abolition activist⟯ ⟮c:10;12 years⟯ later. It ⟮c:11;kept most of the substance⟯, but ⟮c:12;invented⟯ ⟮c:13;hostility from the audience:: attitude⟯, and ⟮c:14;embellished⟯ ⟮c:15;some details⟯ 
the most common version of the famous speech "⟮c:16;Ain't I a woman⟯" has the problem that ⟮c:17;Soujourner Truth⟯ would not have ⟮c:18;spoken in a southern dialect (having grown up in new york as a native dutch speaker⟯), that the ⟮c:19;original transcript did not include the phrase "Ain't I a woman",⟯ and that ⟮c:20;the speech was originally not titled at all⟯ 
  
 The most famous passage of the probably false rendition of "⟮c:21;Ain't I a woman⟯":  
⟮c:22;That man over there⟯ ⟮c:23;says that women need to be⟯ ⟮c:24;helped into carriages⟯, ⟮c:25;and lifted over ditches⟯, ⟮c:26;and to have the best place everywhere⟯. ⟮c:27;Nobody ever⟯ ⟮c:28;helps me into carriages⟯, ⟮c:29;or over mud-puddles⟯, or gives me any best place! And ain't I a woman? Look at me! Look at my arm! I have ploughed and planted, and gathered into barns, and no man could head me! And ain't I a woman? I could work as much and eat as much as a man - when I could get it - and bear the lash as well! And ain't I a woman?&nbsp; 

### waste

⟮c:1;before the advent of sewer systems⟯ ⟮c:2;human waste⟯ either went in ⟮c:3;the river⟯ or in ⟮c:4;cesspits or similar contraptions⟯ 

In ⟮c:2;Berlin⟯, the instituted system for ⟮c:3;the disposal of waste⟯ before ⟮c:4;the 1860s⟯ was&nbsp;⟮c:1;The Rinnsteine⟯ 
The ⟮c:5;Rinnsteine⟯ in Berlin were placed at ⟮c:11;both sides⟯ of the streets, were ⟮c:6;50cm⟯ deep and ⟮c:7;50cm - 1m⟯ wide. 
The ⟮c:8;Rinnsteine⟯ were supposed to move the waste into ⟮c:9;the spree⟯, but did not always succeed due to ⟮c:10;having a geringes gefälle⟯.

## misc

### theories of humor/laughter

In ⟮c:1;the incongruity theory of humor⟯, something is funny because ⟮c:2;it somehow violates our expectations⟯ 
In ⟮c:3;the relief theory of laughter⟯, ⟮c:4;laughter/humor is the release of pent-up nervous energy⟯/ 
The ⟮c:5;relief theory of laughter⟯ reliess (explicitly or implicitly) on the metaphor of ⟮c:6;a steam valve⟯ 
The problem with ⟮c:7;the relief theory of laughter⟯ is that ⟮c:8;nervous energy isn't a real thing that one would need to release.⟯ 
⟮c:9;The superiority theory (of humor/laughter⟯) is the theory that ⟮c:10;laughter expresses feelings of superiority⟯ over ⟮c:11;other people⟯ or over ⟮c:11;a former state of ourselves⟯ (main supporters (possibly, depending on how you read them): ⟮c:12;Plato, Hobbes, Descartes⟯ ) 
<div class="cloze-group hide-if-inactive">
  The problem with the ⟮c:13;superoiority theory of humor/laughter:⟯ 
<ol>
<li>since there are cases in ⟮c:14;which one feels superior to someone (i.e. a beggar⟯) but ⟮c:15;feels pity rather than laughs⟯, ⟮c:16;superiority is not sufficient to explain laughter⟯</li>
<li>Since there are cases in which ⟮c:17;one laughts but doesn't feel superior⟯, ⟮c:18;superiority is not necessary to explain laughter⟯</li>
</ol>
</div>

## structuralism

⟮c:1;CLS⟯ elaborated his ⟮c:2;structuralist⟯ approach to myth in  <cite>⟮c:3;The Structural Study of Myth⟯</cite> ⟮(c:4;1955⟯) 
The ⟮c:5;paradox of myth according⟯ to CLS is that ⟮c:6;anything can happen in a myth⟯, but that ⟮c:7;myths are similar across cultures⟯. 
According to CLS, ⟮c:8;myth⟯ is its own form of ⟮c:9;langue⟯, that is, its own ⟮c:10;structuralist system⟯ 
The ⟮c:11;langue⟯ of ⟮c:12;myth⟯ consists of ⟮c:13;mythemes⟯ . 
According to CLS, myths consist of ⟮c:14;pairs of⟯ ⟮c:18;mythemes⟯, generally ⟮c:15;one⟯ ⟮c:14;s:15;pair⟯ that is ⟮c:16;contradictiory/irreconcilable⟯, and ⟮c:15;one⟯ ⟮c:14;s:15;pair⟯ that is ⟮c:17;reconcilable⟯. 
The ⟮c:19;contradictory pair⟯ of mythemes ⟮c:20;is equated with⟯ ⟮c:21;the reconcilable pair,⟯ and so ⟮c:22;the contradiction is dissolved/solved⟯, e.g. ⟮c:23;life:death⟯ ⟮c:24;↔⟯ ⟮c:25;herbivore:hunter⟯ (which is ⟮c:26;reconcilable⟯ via the ⟮c:26;scavenger⟯); ⟮c:27;life:death⟯ ⟮c:28;↔⟯ ⟮c:29;sky:earth⟯ (which is ⟮c:30;reconcilable⟯ via ⟮c:30;mist⟯) 

Both ⟮c:13;mythical thought⟯ and ⟮c:13;science⟯ are ⟮c:14;equally⟯ ⟮c:15;sophisticated⟯ ⟮c:20;systems of thought⟯ according to ⟮c:16;CLS⟯, however, ⟮c:17;science⟯ is ⟮c:18;more effective at⟯ ⟮c:19;acquiring knowledge to harness the world⟯.

###### fats

⟮c:1;Fat⟯ consists of (is an ester of, whatever that means) ⟮c:2;fatty acids⟯ 
⟮c:3;Fatty acids⟯ may either be ⟮c:4;saturated⟯ or ⟮c:5;unsaturated⟯. 
⟮c:6;Unsaturated⟯ ⟮c:7;fatty acids⟯ ones have ⟮c:8;one or more⟯ ⟮c:9;C=C double bonds⟯.⟮c:10;saturated⟯ factty acids have ⟮c:11;no⟯ ⟮c:12;C=C double bonds⟯. 
⟮c:13;Saturated fats⟯ are called that because they have the ⟮c:14;maximum possible number of hydrogen atoms⟯ attached to ⟮c:15;every carbon atom⟯, and thus no ⟮c:16;need (or possibility⟯) for ⟮c:17;double C=C bonds⟯

Unsaturated fats with ⟮c:21;two or more⟯ ⟮c:22;C=C double bond(s⟯) ⟮c:23;polyunsaturated⟯ 
Since ⟮c:24;cis⟯ and ⟮c:25;trans⟯ fats refer to the ⟮c:26;configuration of the molecule⟯ ⟮c:27;around the C=C double bond⟯, only ⟮c:28;unsaturated⟯ fats can be ⟮c:29;cis⟯ or ⟮c:30;trans⟯. 
since an ⟮c:31;unsaturated fatty acid⟯ can have ⟮c:32;more than one double bond⟯, it can be ⟮c:33;both cis and trans⟯ 
There are two ⟮c:34;essential⟯ fatty acids (EFAs) in human nutrition: ⟮c:35;alpha-linolenic acid⟯ (an ⟮c:36;omega-3 fatty acid⟯) and ⟮c:37;linoleic acid⟯ (an ⟮c:38;omega-6 fatty acid⟯). ⟮c:39;Other lipids needed by the body⟯ ⟮c:40;can be synthesized from these and other fats.⟯

######### omega-3

There are three types of {{c17::omega-3}} fatty acids, {{c14::ALA}}, {{c15::EPA}}, and {{c16::DHA}}.
of the {{c11::three}} types of {{c7::omega-3}} fatty acids relevant to human nutrition, only {{c18::ALA}} is {{c9::essential}}.

Of the omega-3 fatty acids, only {{c19::ALA}} is essential since {{c20::EPA}} and {{c20::DHA}} can be {{c8::synthesized from the former}}.
{{c21::EPA/DHA synthesis from ALA}} may be impaired in {{c10::aging}} and generally is not a {{c12::particularly efficient process}}, as such, {{c13::making sure to consume EPA/DHA}} is probably advisable.

#### micro

##### genesis

⟮c:1;Minerals⟯ ⟮c:3;end up in food⟯ via ⟮c:2;rock, soil, and water,⟯ which are ⟮c:4;absorbed by plants as they grow (or e.g. by animals licking rocks⟯), and then ⟮c:5;passed further up the food chain⟯. 
⟮c:6;What minerals we get⟯ from plants/animals depends on ⟮c:7;which they can store⟯, but also ⟮c:8;what the soil/animals further down the food chain had in them⟯. 
⟮c:9;Vitamins⟯ are ⟮c:10;biosynthesized⟯ by ⟮c:11;plants/animals⟯ and then ⟮c:12;absorbed by us⟯. 
⟮c:13;Which vitamins⟯ are ⟮c:14;contained in food⟯ depend on ⟮c:15;what the relevant vegetable or animal can biosythesize⟯. 

##### vitamins

Theoretically, all ⟮c:1;vitamins⟯ are defined to be ⟮c:2;essential nutrients⟯, however ⟮c:3;Vitamin D⟯ can be synthesized if ⟮c:4;exposed to sunlight (specifically UVB rays⟯), and is thus ⟮c:5;an exception⟯ 
Most ⟮c:6;vitamins⟯ are not ⟮c:7;single molecules⟯, but ⟮c:8;groups of related molecules⟯ which are each called ⟮c:9;vitamers⟯ 
Each ⟮c:10;vitamin⟯ is typically used in ⟮c:11;multiple reactions⟯, and therefore ⟮c:12;most have multiple functions⟯. 
⟮c:13;Vitamin B12⟯ is ⟮c:14;the only⟯ vitamin or nutrient ⟮c:15;not available from plant sources.⟯ 
⟮c:16;Vitamins⟯ are generally categorized as either ⟮c:17;water⟯- or ⟮c:18;fat⟯ soluble 
The ⟮c:19;water-soluble vitamins (the 8 B vitamins and vitamin C⟯) are ⟮c:20;easily excreted from the body⟯, thus generally ⟮c:21;hypervitaminosis (if referring to high storage levels⟯) is not an issue, therefore, however, ⟮c:22;consistent consumption is required⟯. 
Of the ⟮c:23;fat-soluble⟯ vitamins, ⟮c:24;A⟯ and ⟮c:25;D⟯ can ⟮c:26;accumulate in the body to a dangerous level⟯, resulting in ⟮c:27;hypervitaminosis⟯ 
⟮c:28;Hypervitaminosis⟯ is a condition of abnormally high ⟮c:29;storage levels (most of the time, although some sources also just use it for any overdose⟯) of ⟮c:30;vitamins⟯ 
In general, ⟮c:31;excess intake⟯ of vitamins is ⟮c:32;unlikely⟯ from ⟮c:33;food sources⟯, but possible via ⟮c:34;supplements⟯

###### vitamin D

⟮c:1;Vitamin D⟯ deficiency is common worldwide, occurring in about a ⟮c:2;1 billion⟯ people, and is surprisingly common in ⟮c:3;europe⟯. 
⟮c:4;Vitamin D⟯ has many function, amongst the most important being ⟮c:5;increasing the absorption⟯ of ⟮c:6;minerals⟯ such as ⟮c:7;calcium, magnesium, and phosphate⟯ 
⟮c:8;Vitamin D⟯ ⟮c:9;deficiency⟯ can cause or excarbate a wide variety of conditions, many featuring ⟮c:10;bone⟯ or ⟮c:11;muscle⟯ ⟮c:12;weakness⟯, and some ⟮c:13;respiratiory infections⟯ as well as ⟮c:14;mental illnesses⟯ 
⟮c:15;Rickets⟯ is a condition that results in ⟮c:16;weak⟯ and ⟮c:17;soft⟯ ⟮c:18;bones⟯ in ⟮c:19;children⟯ 

⟮c:20;Vitamin D⟯ occurs only ⟮c:21;rarely⟯ in food, specifically in ⟮c:22;oily fish⟯ such as ⟮c:23;salmon/herring/mackerel⟯ as well as ⟮c:24;mushrooms⟯ (if these have been ⟮c:25;exposed to UVB ligh ≈ been in the sun⟯) 
While ⟮c:26;Vitamin D⟯ can be synthesized by contact with ⟮c:27;UVB⟯ rays from ⟮c:28;sunlight⟯, many people ⟮c:29;do not do this enough⟯, it is not necessarily possible in ⟮c:30;winter⟯, and may not be ⟮c:31;advisable⟯ due to ⟮c:32;skin cancer risk⟯ 
In the ⟮c:33;US⟯ and some other countries, ⟮c:34;milk⟯ and ⟮c:35;milk substitutes⟯ as well as ⟮c:36;breakfast cereals⟯ are fortified with ⟮c:37;Vitamin D⟯.

###### iron

⟮c:1;Iron⟯ ⟮c:3;from food⟯ comes in two forms, ⟮c:2;heme⟯ ⟮c:1;iron⟯ which can only be gotten from ⟮c:4;animal flesh⟯, and ⟮c:2;non-heme⟯ ⟮c:1;iron⟯ which can also be gotten from ⟮c:5;other sources⟯ 
⟮c:6;Heme⟯ iron is ⟮c:7;better absorbed⟯ by the body than ⟮c:8;non-heme⟯ iron.

###### iodine

⟮c:1;Iodine⟯ ⟮c:2;deficiency⟯ leads to many bad outcomes, many due to the fact that ⟮c:3;iodine⟯ is necessary to ⟮c:4;make one of the thyroid hormones (thyroxine⟯), lack of which leads to ⟮c:5;high levels of thyroid stimulating hormone (TSH⟯), causing ⟮c:6;the thyroid to swell⟯ 
in many countries, ⟮c:7;table salt⟯ is fortified with ⟮c:8;iodine⟯ in the form of ⟮c:9;iodine⟯ ⟮c:10;salts⟯, which has proved effective at preventing ⟮c:11;iodine⟯ ⟮c:12;deficiency⟯ 
In Deutschland kommen Iodverbindungen ⟮c:13;in den Böden⟯ unverändert ⟮c:14;in zu geringen Mengen vor⟯. Durch ⟮c:15;die Iodierung von Speisesalz⟯ ist ⟮c:16;der Iodmangel in Deutschland verringert worden⟯, dennoch besteht in Deutschland bei ⟮c:17;36⟯ % der Bevölkerung ein ⟮c:18;milder⟯ und bei ⟮c:19;21⟯ % ein ⟮c:20;moderater bis schwerer⟯ ⟮c:21;Iodmangel⟯. 

##### choline

⟮c:1;Choline⟯ is available in what feels like ⟮c:2;just about any food⟯, but is genrally highest in ⟮c:3;animal products (meat/fish/dairy/eggs⟯), which is why it is sometimes said ⟮c:4;vegans should pay some attention to it⟯ (prob not necessary tho).

###### testicular self-examination

###### g-spot 

The ⟮c:1;g-spot⟯ is a ⟮c:2;hypothesized⟯ but ⟮c:2;probably nonexistant/socially constructed⟯ area ⟮c:3;within the vagina⟯ on the ⟮c:4;belly-wards⟯ side supposedly responsible for ⟮c:5;strong orgasms.⟯ 

###### reproduction

####### pearl index

⟮c:1;The pearl index⟯ is a method for reporting ⟮c:2;the effectiveness of a birth control method.⟯ 
The pearl index is ⟮c:3;the number of pregnancies⟯ per ⟮c:4;100⟯ that ⟮c:5;the women participating in the trial⟯ experienced in ⟮c:6;a year⟯. 
Specifically, the ⟮c:7;pearl index⟯ is calculated via ⟮c:10;(⟯⟮c:8;n_pregnancies⟯ ⟮c:9;* 12 * 100⟯⟮c:10;) / (⟯⟮c:11;n_women⟯ ⟮c:10;*⟯ ⟮c:11;n_months⟯⟮c:10;⟯) 
It is theoretically possible to recieve a pearl index of ⟮c:12;1200⟯ if ⟮c:13;all women get pregnant in the first month⟯, however the pearl index may serve as t⟮c:14;he risk of getting pregnant in a year⟯ if ⟮c:15;the pregnancy rate is relatively low⟯ 
⟮c:16;Pearl indices⟯ ⟮c:17;vary⟯ based on ⟮c:18;the culture/demographics⟯ of the people being studied, and ⟮c:19;the method of instruction⟯. 
More seriously, since ⟮c:20;the most fertile couples⟯ will ⟮c:21;get pregnant first⟯, and since ⟮c:22;people will get better at using a contraceptive method as time goes on⟯, ⟮c:24;the longer⟯ ⟮c:23;a study runs⟯, ⟮c:24;the lower⟯ ⟮c:23;its reported Pearl index will be⟯.

##### appendix & appendicitis

⟮c:1;Appendicitis⟯ is the english and formally correct name for ⟮c:2;Blinddarmentzündung⟯. 
Der ⟮c:3;Wurmfortsatz des Blinddarms⟯ is the german name for ⟮c:4;appendix⟯. 
⟮c:5;appendicitis⟯ is ⟮c:6;inflammation of⟯ ⟮c:7;the appendix⟯ 
⟮c:8;The inflammation of the appendix that is appendicitis⟯ is often caused by ⟮c:9;a blockage of the appendix⟯. 
⟮c:10;The blockage of the appendix leading to appendicitis⟯ is most commonly due to ⟮c:11;a calcified 'stone' made of feces⟯. Due to this reason, ⟮c:12;a high-fiber diet⟯ reduces ⟮c:13;the long-term risk of appendicitis⟯. 
The risk of getting ⟮c:14;appendicitis⟯ at some point in your life is ⟮c:15;7-8⟯%, most commonly in ⟮c:16;those under 30⟯. 
There is ⟮c:17;a cluster ofsigns&amp;symptoms⟯ associated with ⟮c:18;appendicitis⟯, but ⟮c:19;a fairly high percentage (40%⟯) ⟮c:20;do not present most/all of the typical symptoms⟯, and ⟮c:21;a smaller percentage⟯ may have ⟮c:22;their pain be located differently⟯, due to ⟮c:23;the appendix itself being in a different location⟯. 
The ⟮c:24;pain⟯ for ⟮c:24;appendicitis⟯ generally appears relatively ⟮c:25;suddenly⟯ ⟮c:26;near or above the belly button⟯, ⟮c:27;from where it then moves⟯ ⟮c:28;into the right lower belly⟯. 
The ⟮c:29;pain⟯ for ⟮c:29;appendicitis⟯ is usually ⟮c:30;strong⟯ and ⟮c:31;gets worse⟯ with ⟮c:32;(esp. jarring) movement⟯ (one can test for this by ⟮c:33;jumping up or down⟯). 
⟮c:34;The hamburger sign⟯ is used for ⟮c:35;assessing if the person has appendicitis⟯: the person ⟮c:36;is asked if they would like to eat their favorite food⟯: A ⟮c:37;positive hamburger sign⟯ is ⟮c:38;them declining the food⟯, with ⟮c:39;a sensitivity (true postive rate⟯) of ⟮c:40;80%⟯. 
One of the symptoms of ⟮c:42;appendicitis⟯ is ⟮c:41;stiff⟯ ⟮c:43;belly muscles⟯. Connected to this is ⟮c:44;rebound tenderness⟯: If you ⟮c:45;press on your belly for a while⟯, and then ⟮c:46;suddenly release⟯, the ⟮c:47;main pain⟯ will be ⟮c:47;on release (though the initial press will also hurt⟯) 
To determine the exisitence of ⟮c:48;appendicitis⟯ with certainty, ⟮c:49;CT/ultrasound⟯ is needed. 
If ⟮c:50;appendicitis is left untreated⟯, ⟮c:51;the appendix may burst⟯, causing ⟮c:52;the inflammation of the interior abdominal wall (peritonitis⟯). Once this has happened, there is ⟮c:53;a high risk of death⟯. 
⟮c:54;Appendicitis⟯ is also often accomodated by ⟮c:55;nausea⟯ and ⟮c:55;vomiting⟯.

##### thrombi

What we call ⟮c:3;a blood clot⟯ is more properly called ⟮c:4;thrombus⟯. 
⟮c:2;Thrombosis⟯ is the formation of ⟮c:12;a thrombus⟯ ⟮c:1;inside a blood vessel⟯ that ⟮c:5;obstructs the flow of blood⟯. 
If ⟮c:7;the thrombus then breaks free⟯, it becomes an ⟮c:8;embolus⟯. 
if ⟮c:13;a thrombus⟯, after ⟮c:14;breaking free and becoming an embolus⟯ ⟮c:9;get lodged again⟯, that is a form ⟮c:10;embolism⟯, specifically a ⟮s:10;((c:11;::thromboembolism⟯)). 
Based on where they occur, ⟮c:15;thromboses⟯ are classified into ⟮c:6;venous⟯ and ⟮c:6;arterial⟯. 

###### thromboses

⟮c:3;Thromboses⟯ most often occur in ⟮c:2;the veins⟯ of ⟮c:1;the legs⟯, other bodyparts are ⟮c:4;affected far more rarely⟯. 
If you have a ⟮c:5;thrombosis⟯, your limb, esp. your leg, will often become some of the following:&nbsp; ⟮c:7;swollen::size⟯, ⟮c:8;turn red or blue::color⟯, ⟮c:9;have more visible veins::appearance of thing⟯, ⟮c:10;be shiny due to taut::surface appearance⟯, ⟮c:11;may feel more heavy::feeling⟯, ⟮c:12;be warmer::feeling⟯, and ⟮c:13;hurt quite severely, especially while stepping::pain⟯. 
⟮c:6;Thromboses⟯ are often caused by ⟮c:14;not moving that bodypart for a while⟯, e.g. ⟮c:15;because of an illness that prevents it⟯ 
If ⟮c:17;a thrombus blocks the blood vessel completely⟯, ⟮c:16;the surrounding tissue will start to die⟯.

##### emboli

An ⟮c:1;embolus⟯ is ⟮c:2;some kind of foreign object⟯ (amongst others: ⟮s:1-2;((c:3;::thrombi⟯, ⟮c:4;fat globules, gas bubbles, cholesterol plaque/crystals⟯)) ) that ⟮c:5;travels through the bloodstream⟯. 
If an ⟮c:11;embolus⟯ ⟮c:6;gets lodged⟯ and thus ⟮s:6;((c:7;::blocks a blood vessel⟯)), this is known as an ⟮c:8;embolism⟯. ⟮s:6-8;If it's ((c:9;::a thrombus⟯ that ⟮c:9;gets lodged⟯, it is known as a ⟮c:10;thromboembolism⟯.)) 

###### pulmonary embolism

The ⟮c:1;severity of the effects⟯ of ⟮c:2;an embolism⟯ vary based on ⟮c:3;location (in the body⟯). 
One of ⟮c:4;the most severe types⟯ of embolism is ⟮c:5;pulmonary embolism⟯, i.e. ⟮c:6;embolism in the lungs⟯.

### disorder

⟮c:30;TBDSSMFM⟯ may be used as ⟮c:31;any of the 'big' word types (lexical categories), i.e. noun, verb, adjective, adverb, etc.⟯

The ⟮c:47;distinction between the different parts of TBDSSMFM⟯ is ⟮c:48;most clear in the BDSM community⟯

## phenomena

### violence

#### behavioral and structural

⟮c:1;Structural violence⟯ is ⟮c:2;the tangible harm⟯ done by ⟮c:3;social structures⟯. 
⟮c:6;behavioral violence⟯ is ⟮c:4;the tangible harm⟯ done ⟮c:5;by agents volitionally⟯ 
⟮c:7;Structural violence⟯ is contrasted with ⟮c:8;behavioral violence⟯. 
However, in a second analysis, we might argue that ⟮c:9;we can reduce behavioral violence to structural violence⟯, for example via the concepts of ⟮c:10;stochastic/scripted terrorism/violence⟯ 
⟮c:11;about 300k deaths in a year in the US are attributable to income inequality⟯ is an example of ⟮c:12;structural violence⟯ 
⟮c:13;"Over 20 years’ worth of studies show that people of color who arrive at a hospital while having a heart attack are significantly less likely to receive aspirin, beta-blocking drugs, clot-dissolving drugs, acute cardiac catheterization, angioplasty, or bypass surgery."⟯ is an example of ⟮c:14;structural violence⟯ 

##### stochastic violence

⟮c:1;Stochastic violence⟯ is the idea of ⟮c:2;spreading a violent message via mass communication⟯, which ⟮c:3;makes resulting acts of violence⟯ ⟮c:5;probable⟯, but ⟮c:4;the actual place and time of the occurrence are⟯ ⟮c:5;random⟯. 
⟮c:6;Stochastic violence⟯ used for ⟮c:7;terrorism⟯ is called ⟮c:8;stochastic terrorism⟯ 
By ⟮c:9;transforming⟯ ⟮c:10;lone wolves⟯ ⟮c:9;into⟯ ⟮c:10;instances of larger patterns of violent messages⟯, ⟮c:11;stochastic violence⟯ ⟮c:12;unmasks behavioural violence⟯ as ⟮c:13;structural violence⟯ (perhaps) 
⟮c:14;Scripted violence⟯ is where ⟮c:15;someone describes the kind of violence they want to be carried out⟯, and then ⟮c:16;leaves it up to the audience to carry out this violence⟯. It may be seen as an instace of ⟮c:17;attempted stochastic violence/terrorism⟯

#### economics

##### pay gap

There are two forms of ⟮c:5;x pay/wage gap⟯, ⟮c:6;non-adjusted⟯ and ⟮c:7;adjusted.⟯ 
The ⟮c:8;non-adjusted⟯ ⟮c:10;pay gap⟯ {{c11::just takes into account the difference in average pay} 
The ⟮c:12;adjusted⟯ ⟮c:9;pay gap⟯ ⟮c:13;adjusts for factors such as employee's age, education level, years of work experience, occupation, industry, location, company and job title⟯ 
The pay gap that it was coined for was the ⟮c:14;gender pay gap⟯, there is however also ⟮c:15;strong evidence for pay gaps for other - groups,⟯ such as ⟮c:16;the race pay⟯ gap. 
For ⟮c:17;intersectional identities⟯, ⟮c:18;pay gaps⟯ can be ⟮c:19;stark indeed⟯, for example, ⟮c:20;black women⟯ are payed ⟮c:21;63 cents⟯ and ⟮c:22;latina women⟯ just ⟮c:23;55 cents⟯ (both ⟮c:24;non-adjusted) ⟯ for ⟮c:25;each dollar a white man makes.⟯ 

##### ceiling

the x ceiling is the unofficial/uncodified/'invisible' limit on career advancement that people with a certain - identity face.
heterosexism|lavender ceiling
sexism|glass ceiling
racism (asian-american)|bamboo ceiling
nativisim (refugees)|canvas ceiling
classism|class ceiling

Importantly, the cotton ceiling is the exclusion of trans people from consideration as sexual partners, not anything to do with employment.

###### cliff

the ⟮c:1;x cliff⟯ is the idea that even if ⟮c:2;people with a - identity⟯) ⟮s:4;s:5;((c:3;::break through the glass ceiling⟯)), they are often given ⟮c:4;executive positions⟯ during ⟮c:5;periods of crisis/downturn⟯, where ⟮s:5; ((c:6;::the risk of failure is highest⟯, e.g. ⟮s:6;c:7;Ellen Pao at reddit, Thresa May after brexit.⟯)) 

#### culture

x culture, where x is an aspect of the - experience is the pervasiveness and normalization of x.
x culture often gives rise to victim-blaming.

similarly but slightly differently, ⟮c:1;Car culture⟯ (when it doesn't just mean ⟮c:2;an appreciation for cars⟯) refers to ⟮c:3;a culture/environment/society which is designed for and around cars.⟯

#### victim-blaming

Victim blaming occurs when the victim of a crime or any wrongful act is held entirely or partially at fault for the harm that befell them.
Victim blaming is common when someone has been victim of violence based on one's - experience.

## more than one identity

### intersectionality

⟮c:1;intersectionality⟯ considers ⟮c:2;social identities⟯ and concomittant ⟮c:3;oppression/domination⟯ and ⟮c:3;privilege⟯ in ⟮c:4;combination⟯, rather than ⟮c:5;considering each factor in isolation⟯. 
⟮c:16;Intersectionality⟯ was coined by ⟮c:6;Crenshaw⟯ in ⟮c:7;1989⟯ and started ⟮c:8;being popular as a framework⟯ in ⟮c:9;the early 2000s⟯ 
In 1989, Kimberlé Crenshaw coined the term "⟮c:10;intersectionality⟯" in a paper as a way to help explain the ⟮c:11;oppression of African-American women⟯. She argues the experience of being a ⟮c:12;black woman⟯ ⟮c:13;cannot be understood⟯ in terms ⟮c:14;independent of either being black or a woman⟯. Rather, it must include ⟮c:15;interactions between the two identities⟯, which, she adds, frequently reinforce one another. 
⟮c:17;Tripple oppression⟯ is a term developed originally by ⟮c:18;black socialists in the US⟯, which refers to (in our modern terminolgy) ⟮c:19;the intersectional experience⟯ of ⟮c:20;black women⟯ at the nexus of ⟮c:20;classism, racism, and sexism⟯

⟮h:all;<img src="sm_2021-10-31--17-32-33-screenshot.jpg">⟯A ⟮c:1;fear ladder⟯ is a technique within ⟮c:2;exposure therapy⟯. 
For any specific ⟮c:3;fear/anxiety/phobia⟯, a ⟮c:11;fear ladder⟯ consists of ⟮c:4;specific situations/steps⟯ and ⟮c:5;their associated fear ratings⟯ 
⟮c:12;Fear ratings⟯ for fears on ⟮c:12;the fear ladder⟯ generally are on ⟮c:6;a scale from 1 to 10⟯ 
There should roughly be ⟮c:13;one situation/action⟯ on the fear ladder for ⟮c:14;each fear rating from 1 to 10⟯. 
A ⟮c:15;fear ladder⟯ should be applied ⟮c:16;regularly⟯ (e.g. ⟮s:16;((c:17;::daily, a few times a week⟯)) ). 
⟮c:18;Each session of the application of a fear ladder⟯ should last until ⟮c:19;the thing feels less anxiety-provoking::desired outcome?⟯ ( ⟮s:19;((c:20;::half⟯ is a good goal to short for)) ) (often ⟮c:21;30min +::length⟯ are necessary, ⟮c:7;relaxation excersizes such as breathing⟯ can help) 
⟮c:22;Fear ladders &amp; exposure therapy⟯ work(s) because ⟮c:8;your anxiety basically "runs out of gas"⟯. For ⟮c:23;a fear ladder⟯, start with ⟮c:9;the lower items⟯ on ⟮c:23;the fear ladder,⟯ and ⟮c:9;work yourself up to the higher items⟯ as they ⟮c:10;become less frightening⟯.

### OCD

OCD|Obsessive-compulsive disorder.
<article>§§ ⟮c:2;OCD⟯ (at least using current definitions and stats) affects ⟮c:1;about 2%⟯ of people at ⟮c:3;some point in their life.⟯ §<br>
  §§ ⟮c:4;The onset⟯ of ⟮c:5;OCD⟯ is most commonly ⟮c:6;in late adolescense/early 20s.⟯ §<br></article>
  <article>§§ In general, ⟮c:7;OCD⟯ mainly consists of ⟮c:1;obsessions⟯ and {{c-::compulsions}} §<br>
⟮c:8;Obsessions⟯ are ⟮c:9;intrusive = non-voluntary⟯, ⟮c:10;disturbing/uncomfortable/scary⟯ ⟮c:11;thoughts⟯. 
⟮c:12;Compulsions⟯ are ⟮c:13;the acts performed⟯ to ⟮c:14;soothe the obsessions⟯. 
People generally carry out their ⟮c:16;compulsions⟯ in a ⟮c:15;very structured⟯ manner. 
  §§ People suffering from OCD generally ⟮c:17;do not want to⟯ ⟮c:18;perform the compulsions⟯, ⟮c:19;but the discomfort of the compulsion and the need to get relief is often stronger⟯. §<br></article>
  <article>§§ If acute, compulsions may take up ⟮c:20;a hour or more⟯ of the day. §<br>
OCD-like thoughts are ⟮c:21;common for everyone⟯, the question is ⟮c:22;the severity⟯. 
The ⟮c:23;most common type⟯ of ⟮c:24;OCD obsession⟯ is ⟮c:25;fears related to contamination (e.g. dirt, germs, viruses, chemicals⟯). 
  §§ Sometimes ⟮c:26;the obsession will become less important over time⟯, and ⟮c:27;the compusion will be carried out more as a habit⟯. §<br></article>

#### Strategy to get people to understand OCD

<ol>
<li>⟮c:1;Ask relevant person to agree with a statement that saying something or thinking something doesn’t mean it will come true, that our thoughts are not magical and can’t make things happen⟯</li>
<li>⟮c:2;s:1;Have them write down something terrible happen to a loved one⟯</li>

## motivation

### learned helplessness

In aquiring ⟮c:1;learned helplessness⟯, agents are exposed to ⟮c:2;an averse stimulus⟯ that ⟮c:3;they cannot control/avoid⟯ ⟮c:4;repeatedly⟯ 
With ⟮c:5;learned helplessness⟯, agents fail to f⟮c:6;ail to avoid the thingy⟯ after ⟮c:7;being exposed to an unavoidable averse stimulus⟯, even when ⟮c:8;they have control again (and this is indicated clearly⟯) 
In fact, in contrast to the ⟮c:9;original idea of learned helplessness⟯, it seems that ⟮c:10;passivity is the default reaction⟯ and ⟮c:11;'helpfulness' is learned⟯ 
The ⟮c:12;original⟯ ⟮c:13;learned helplessness⟯ ⟮c:12;experiment⟯ featured ⟮c:14;shocking dogs⟯ ⟮c:15;without them being able to do anything about it.⟯ 
In ⟮c:16;the original learned helplessness experiment⟯, one needed to ⟮c:17;physically show the dogs (by moving their limbs⟯) ⟮c:18;how to escape the shocks⟯ ⟮c:19;at least twice⟯ so ⟮c:20;they would relearn that they had control⟯ 
The ⟮c:21;noise experiment⟯ is related to ⟮c:22;learned helplessness⟯ 
⟮c:36;Hiroto, D.S.; Seligman, M.E.P.⟯ ⟮(c:37;1975⟯), subjects were supposed to ⟮c:38;do mental tasks⟯ while ⟮c:39;a distracting sound played⟯. ⟮h:gb;((c:40;::One experimental group⟯ ⟮c:41;could (or thought they could) turn it off⟯, and ⟮c:42;the other⟯ ⟮c:43;couldn't⟯. ⟮h:gb;((c:44;::The ones that could turn of the noise⟯ ⟮c:45;did better on the task⟯ ⟮c:46;even if they didn't turn it off.⟯)))) 
⟮c:23;Self-efficacy⟯ is ⟮c:24;(The strength of) one's belief⟯ in ⟮c:25;one's own ability⟯ to ⟮c:26;complete asks in order to reach a goal⟯ 
⟮c:27;Veritasium⟯ argues students/employees will ⟮c:28;try their best(based on what they know⟯) when ⟮c:29;given a task⟯, but ⟮c:30;if they hit a roadblock⟯, they ⟮c:31;won't try anything else (but just ask the supervisor⟯) - even though t⟮c:32;hey could have probably figured out methods to overcome a roadblock⟯ - reflecting, according to Veritasium, ⟮c:33;learned helplessness⟯ 
Veritasium argues besides the pracitcal reasons of connection and resources etc, ⟮c:34;self-efficacy⟯ is part of the reason ⟮c:35;children of people with a certain career take up that career.⟯ 

## relationships

### gaslighting

We get the term ⟮c:1;gaslighting⟯ from ⟮c:2;the 1940s film Gaslight⟯ 
In the film ⟮c:3;Gaslight⟯, the protagonist ⟮c:4;claims the gas lights were not dimmed⟯ after ⟮c:5;dimming them himself.⟯ 
⟮c:6;The protagonists goal⟯ in the film ⟮c:7;gaslight⟯ is ⟮c:8;to have his wife declared insane⟯. 
⟮c:9;Gaslighting⟯ is a form of ⟮c:10;manipulation⟯. 
The effect of (successful) ⟮c:11;gaslighting⟯ is that the victim ⟮c:12;can no longer determine⟯ /⟮c:12;is mislead about⟯ ⟮c:13;the trut⟯h, or that they ⟮c:14;can no longer tell⟯ ⟮c:15;right⟯ from ⟮c:15;wrong⟯, ⟮c:16;beneficial⟯ from ⟮c:16;harmful⟯. 
For example, someone ⟮c:17;gaslighting⟯ may deny that ⟮c:18;past events⟯ ⟮c:19;have happen⟯, and thus ⟮c:20;make the gaslit person question if they actually have⟯. 
For example, someone ⟮c:21;gaslighting⟯ may ⟮c:22;make fun of someones needs⟯, and thus ⟮c:23;make them question if their needs are actually legitimate⟯. 
⟮c:24;Gaslighting⟯ may be ⟮c:25;intentional⟯ or ⟮c:25;unintentional⟯, and may be more often used by ⟮c:26;narcissists / sociopaths⟯. 
⟮c:27;Gaslighting⟯ can occur in ⟮c:28;any personal relationship⟯, we may also want to ⟮c:29;extend the definition to a societal level⟯.

### dark triad

#### narcissism

{{c1::<dfn>Narcissists</dfn>}} have an overriding desire to {{c2::protect/soothe/strengthen}} their {{c3::self-image.}} 
{{c4::<dfn>Narcisstic supply</dfn>}} are {{c58::the doses of admiration, validation, approval, respect, attention, subservience}} that {{c57::a narcisst seeks to soothe their self-image.}} 
<dfn>{{c5::Narcisstic injury}}</dfn> is {{c6::damage}} that a narcisst takes {{c7::to their self-image.}} 

In ⟮c:8;a family with a narcissist⟯, people end up ⟮c:9;being forced into roles⟯ - ⟮c:10;the whole construct⟯ is called the <dfn>⟮c:11;narcissistic family⟯</dfn>. 
⟮c:17;The narcisstic family⟯'s ⟮c:12;roles⟯ are ⟮c:13;structural⟯: ⟮c:14;The relationships between roles is what creates the structure, and makes it resilient to change.⟯ 
⟮c:16;The narcissistic family⟯ consists of different ⟮c:18;roles⟯: ⟮c:19;the narcissist⟯, ⟮c:20;the golden child⟯ and ⟮c:21;the scapegoat⟯ ⟮c:22;establish the structure⟯, butressed by ⟮c:23;flying monkeys⟯, ⟮c:24;neutral siblings/people.⟯ and sometimes also others (e.g. enablers)
⟮c:25;Roles⟯ in a ⟮c:26;narcissistic family⟯ may ⟮c:27;shift⟯ if ⟮c:28;they do not provide the function to the narcissist anymore.⟯ 

In a narcissistic family the ⟮c:29;<dfn>golden child</dfn>⟯ is the person ⟮c:30;onto which the narcissist projects⟯ what ⟮c:31;they want to believe about themselves.⟯ 
The ⟮c:32;golden child⟯ ⟮c:33;strengthens the narcissists self-image⟯ by ⟮c:34;projection⟯. 
The ⟮c:35;golden child⟯'s ⟮c:36;achievements⟯ are ⟮c:37;highly rewarded⟯. 

In a narcissistic family, the <dfn>⟮c:38;scapegoat⟯</dfn> is the person ⟮c:39;onto which the narcissist projects⟯ what t⟮c:40;hey don't want to believe about themselves.⟯ 
The ⟮c:41;scapegoat⟯ ⟮c:42;strengthens the narcissists self-image⟯ by ⟮c:43;contrast⟯. 
The ⟮c:44;scapegoat⟯'s ⟮c:45;achievements⟯ are ⟮c:46;not acknowledged⟯, and may ⟮c:46;even surreptitiosuly be punished⟯. 
Conversely, the ⟮c:47;scapegoat⟯'s ⟮c:48;failures⟯ may be ⟮c:49;rewarded⟯. 
However, the scapegoat may have an easier time ⟮c:50;breaking away from the narcisstic family structure⟯. 

In a narcisstic family, the ⟮c:51;<dfn>neutral sibling/person</dfn>⟯ is said to be the sibling/person that ⟮c:52;always tries to mediate⟯, but ends up ⟮c:53;denying the reality of what's happening⟯. 
In a narcisstic family, a <dfn>⟮c:54;flying monkey⟯</dfn> is person ⟮c:55;used by the narcissist⟯ as ⟮c:56;a proxy for their narcissist actions.⟯

### more than one category

#### radicals

#### ions

An ⟮c:1;ion⟯ is a particle, atom or molecule with a ⟮c:2;net electrical charge⟯.
A ⟮c:3;cation⟯ is a ⟮c:4;positively⟯ charged ion with ⟮c:5;fewer⟯ ⟮c:6;electrons⟯ ⟮c:5;than⟯ ⟮c:6;protons⟯
A ⟮c:7;anion⟯ is a ⟮c:8;negatively⟯ charged ion with ⟮c:9;more⟯ ⟮c:10;electrons⟯ ⟮c:9;than⟯ ⟮c:10;protons⟯
Because of ⟮c:11;their opposite electric charges⟯, ⟮c:12;cations⟯ and ⟮c:12;anions⟯ ⟮c:13;attract each other⟯ and readily form ⟮c:14;ionic compounds⟯.

# lock-picking

## lockpicking tools

In general, the three major types of lockpicking tools are ⟮c:1;hooks⟯, ⟮c:2;rakes⟯, and ⟮c:3;tensioning tools⟯.

⟮c:20;Pin tumbler locks⟯ are the most common type of ⟮c:21;mechanism⟯ for ⟮c:22;cylinder locks⟯. 
⟮c:23;A pin tumbler lock⟯ uses ⟮c:24;pins⟯ ⟮c:25;contained in a tumbler⟯ to ⟮c:26;prevent locks from opening⟯. 
⟮c:27;Pin tumbler locks⟯ are the ones we think of when we think of ⟮c:28;locks and lock-picking⟯.

## shear line

⟮h:1;<img src="sm_200px-Pin_tumbler_bypass.svg.png">⟯
⟮h:1;<img src="sm_How-a-Lock-Works-Animation.gif">⟯
<img class="all-f" src="sm_Simple-Paper-Lock-Lock-Picking-Theory.gif">

<img src="Pin-Tumbler-Lock-Shear-Line.png"><br/>The ⟮c:1;shear line⟯ is ⟮c:2;the gap between⟯ ⟮c:3;the casing⟯ and ⟮c:3;the plug⟯ 
⟮c:4;the driver pins⟯ are the pins that ⟮c:5;the springs are directly contactin⟯g 
⟮c:6;the key pins⟯ are the pins that ⟮c:7;the key will directly contact⟯ and that ⟮c:8;thus 'read' the key⟯ 
To ⟮c:9;open a door⟯, ⟮c:10;the plug⟯ ⟮c:11;must be able to rotate⟯. 
If a door is ⟮c:12;locked⟯, ⟮c:13;the pins⟯ are ⟮c:14;preventing the plug from rotating⟯. 
For the ⟮c:17;plug to be able to rotate⟯, ⟮c:15;the gaps between pins⟯ must ⟮c:16;line up with the shear line.⟯ 
If ⟮c:18;the correct key is inserted⟯, it makes ⟮c:19;the gaps between pins⟯ ⟮c:20;line up with the shear line⟯. 
To ⟮c:21;prevent any old key to be used to open a lock⟯, ⟮c:22;the key pins have different lengths⟯ 
If you ⟮c:23;insert the wrong key into a lock⟯, the lock will not open because ⟮c:24;gaps between pins not in line with shear line⟯ 
The part of the lock that we are exploiting to pick is ⟮c:25;the shear line⟯ 
The thing that ⟮c:26;keeps the pins down in a lock⟯ are ⟮c:27;the springs⟯.

## manufacturing tolerances

<img class="all-b c1-f c2-f c3-f" src="sm_images-9ed43522ef2c55350dcd6adefc4db944373d3d89.png">
⟮h:all;<img src="sm_Real-Plug-Lock-Flaws.jpg">⟯In a general sense, locks would ⟮c:4;not be pickable⟯ if not for ⟮c:5;manufacturing tolerances⟯. 
The reason that ⟮c:6;not all pins bind⟯ once you ⟮c:7;apply rotational tension⟯ is tha⟮c:8;t the holes are ever-so-slightly off-center⟯. 
The pin that will ⟮c:1;bind here first⟯ if we ⟮c:2;rotate in the upwards direction⟯ is ⟮c:3;the second or the fourth pin.⟯ 

## binding pins

⟮h:all;<img src="sm_Setting-Standard-Pin-Lock-Picking-Theory-.gif">⟯
⟮h:all;<img src="sm_Lock-Picking-Single-Pin-Picking.gif">⟯
⟮h:all;<img src="sm_Peterson-GSP-Hook-1-Black-Handle.jpg">⟯A ⟮c:8;bound⟯ pin is the one that if ⟮c:9;rotational tension is being applied⟯ is ⟮c:10;hard to move (won't slip⟯) 
⟮c:11;Applying rotational tension⟯ allows ⟮c:12;pins⟯ to ⟮c:13;bind⟯, and thus ⟮c:14;setting them⟯. 
Pins can only ⟮c:15;bind⟯ if ⟮c:16;rotational tension is being applied⟯, otherwise ⟮c:17;they won't get stuck⟯ and f⟮c:18;all right back into the cylinder.⟯ 
There is ⟮c:21;most often (though not always⟯) ⟮c:19;only one⟯ ⟮c:20;binding pin⟯ at a time. 
§§ The ⟮c:22;binding pin⟯ is ⟮c:23;the pin⟯ that ⟮c:24;is currently being bound⟯ (or ⟮c:24;will be bound once rotational tension is applied⟯), and thus is ⟮c:25;the next pin that can be set.⟯ §<br>

⟮c:26;Single pin picking⟯ is ⟮c:27;picking the pins one at a time⟯, which looks something like ⟮c:30;&lt;image above&gt;⟯ 
⟮c:28;Single pin picking⟯ uses ⟮c:29;hooks⟯ as tools, which look something like ⟮c:31;&lt;image&gt;⟯ 
Core loop of ⟮c:32;single pin picking⟯: ⟮c:33;find binding pin⟯ -&gt; ⟮c:34;set binding pin⟯ 
To ⟮c:35;test if⟯ ⟮c:36;sth is a binding pin⟯, you ⟮c:37;push it slightly⟯. 
Compared to ⟮c:38;non-binding pins⟯, if we push on ⟮c:38;the binding pin⟯, it will feel ⟮c:39;slightly stiffer and harder to lift⟯. 
T⟮c:40;o set the binding pin⟯, we ⟮c:41;push on the pin⟯ until ⟮c:42;we reach the shear line⟯. 
Once ⟮c:43;you've pushed the binding pin to the shear line⟯, if ⟮c:44;you've set the pin⟯, ⟮c:45;the driver pin will catch⟯ and t⟮c:46;he key pin will fall into the plug⟯. 
Once ⟮c:47;you've pushed the binding pin to the shear line⟯, if y⟮c:48;ou've set the pin⟯, you probably hear ⟮c:49;a slight click⟯ or feel ⟮c:50;a slight rotation⟯. 
§§ However, it is also possible to §<br>  <tbody class="cloze-group-children hide-if-inactive-children">
⟮c:1;push the binding pin in too far⟯|⟮c:2;overset⟯|⟮c:3;<img src="sm_overset_1130.jpg">⟯
⟮c:4;not push the binding pin in far enough⟯|⟮c:5;underset⟯|⟮c:6;<img src="sm_underset_1130.jpg">⟯

If you ⟮c:51;can no longer find a binding pin⟯, you have likely ⟮c:7;overset or underset a pin⟯ 

## tensioning

<span class="c1-f">What is this tool?</span><br><img class="c1-f all-b" src="sm_standard-tension-wrench.jpg"><br>⟮c:3;tensioning tools⟯ are for ⟮c:4;applying tension to the lock (to bind the pins⟯) 
The ⟮c:5;tension wrench⟯ is ⟮c:6;the most common type of⟯ ⟮c:7;tensioning tool.⟯ 
A tension wrench might look like ⟮c:2;&lt;image&gt;⟯ 
A ⟮c:8;tension wrench⟯ has ⟮c:9;a short end⟯ to ⟮c:10;place in the keyway⟯ and ⟮c:9;a long end⟯ to ⟮c:10;act as a lever⟯. 
You need to apply ⟮c:11;very little tension⟯ with ⟮c:12;the tension wrench⟯ to bind the pins. 
⟮c:13;Letting go of the tension applied with the tension wrench⟯ ⟮c:14;resets the lock⟯. 

## raking

<span class="c1-f">What are we doing here?</span><span class="c21-f">What is this tool?</span><br/><img class="all-b c1-f" src="sm_Lock-Picking-With-A-Bogota-Rake-2.gif"><img class="c20-b" src="sm_Peterson-GSP-Bogie-2-Black-Handle.jpg"><br/>⟮c:2;Raking⟯ is ⟮c:3;scrubbing the pins⟯ until we ⟮c:4;happen to set all the pins⟯. 
Raking might look like ⟮c:5;&lt;image&gt;⟯ 
⟮c:6;Raking⟯ has roughly the same motion as ⟮c:7;brushing your teeth⟯. 
In raking, we ⟮c:8;add tension⟯ and then ⟮c:9;scrub the pins⟯. 
In raking, while ⟮c:10;scrubbing the pins⟯, ⟮c:11;vary⟯ things like ⟮c:12;speed, tension, angle, height, etc⟯ ⟮c:11;slightly⟯. 
In raking, we ⟮c:13;reset the lock⟯ by ⟮c:14;removing tension.⟯ 
In ⟮c:15;raking⟯, we often have to ⟮c:16;reset the lock a few times⟯ before it works 
⟮c:17;Raking⟯ has the main advantage of ⟮c:18;being fast (to open the lock⟯), but the disadvantage of ⟮c:19;not working well for more difficult locks⟯. 
⟮c:23;Raking⟯ uses ⟮c:22;rakes⟯ as tools, which look like ⟮c:20;&lt;image&gt;⟯
